This is already a much cleaner reduction than the earlier primitive-area language.

Now there are three exact consequences.

First, repeated tangent slope is exactly
T(u1)=T(u2)  ⟺  M1(u1)=M1(u2).
T(u1​)=T(u2​)⟺M1​(u1​)=M1​(u2​).

So the tangent-slope return problem is nothing but return of the single scalar cumulative moment M1M1​.

Second, since
S′(u)=4u3M3(u),
S′(u)=u34​M3​(u),

the cusp-secant slope is strictly monotone whenever M3M3​ keeps one sign. So secant return from the cusp point can happen only if M3M3​ changes sign or vanishes.

Third, the tangent-at-uu passing through a later point v>uv>u is governed by
H(u,v):=y(v)−y(u)−T(u)(x(v)−x(u)).
H(u,v):=y(v)−y(u)−T(u)(x(v)−x(u)).

Using x′(s)=2sx′(s)=2s and T=2M1T=2M1​,
H(u,v)=∫uv2s (T(s)−T(u)) ds=4∫uvs (M1(s)−M1(u)) ds.
H(u,v)=∫uv​2s(T(s)−T(u))ds=4∫uv​s(M1​(s)−M1​(u))ds.

So if M1M1​ is strictly monotone, then H(u,v)H(u,v) has fixed sign for every v>uv>u, and the tangent at uu never passes through a later point.

So the whole quotient-cusp branch is globally sterile if you can prove:
M1 is strictly monotone and M3 has one sign on the relevant branch.
M1​ is strictly monotone and M3​ has one sign on the relevant branch.​

That is the cleanest theorem target I see now.

It is also the minimal useful target. You do not need full control of KK, and you do not need to solve for G~G
. It is enough to control these two weighted cumulative moments.

There is one more honest refinement: abstractly, this is still not enough to close the lane without provenance input. You can build KK with +,−,++,−,+ sign pattern so that M1M1​ has a return and M3M3​ changes sign, and then all the bad quotient-cusp geometry is realized. So this is not a formal consequence of cusp shape alone.

That means the next live theorem is now very specific:
prove a provenance-driven sign/monotonicity law for M1(u)=∫0usK(s) ds and M3(u)=∫0us3K(s) ds.
prove a provenance-driven sign/monotonicity law for M1​(u)=∫0u​sK(s)ds and M3​(u)=∫0u​s3K(s)ds.​

If I were pushing next, I would try to extract exactly one of these from the exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 residue:

    M1′(u)=uK(u)M1′​(u)=uK(u) has fixed sign on the quotient branch,

    or M3(u)M3​(u) has fixed sign on the quotient branch,

    or a one-sided comparison such as
    M3(u)=u2M1(u)−u22S(u)
    M3​(u)=u2M1​(u)−2u2​S(u)

    together with a provenance constraint on SS that forces the sign of M3M3​.

So the attack has now reduced the residual quotient-cusp lane to a very sharp pair of scalar moment laws.

continue the attack

Yes. There is a real simplification.

On the quotient cusp branch, you do not need to control both M1M1​ and M3M3​ independently.
A one-sided monotonicity law for M1M1​ alone already forces the right behavior of M3M3​.

That is the cleanest further reduction I can justify.

Let
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G~(u)),u≥0,
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G
(u)),u≥0,

and define
T(u):=y′(u)x′(u)=G~′(u)2u,S(u):=y(u)x(u)=G~(u)u2,

′(u)​,S(u):=x(u)y(u)​=u2G
(u)​,

and

K(u):=dxdT​(u)=ϕμμ​(u).

As before, set

M1​(u):=∫0u​sK(s)ds,M3​(u):=∫0u​s3K(s)ds.

Then

T(u)=2M1​(u),T(u)−S(u)=u22​M3​(u).

Now the key identity is:
M3(u)=u2M1(u)−2∫0us M1(s) ds.
M3​(u)=u2M1​(u)−2∫0u​sM1​(s)ds.​

This is just integration by parts:
M3(u)=∫0us2 dM1(s)=u2M1(u)−∫0u2s M1(s) ds.
M3​(u)=∫0u​s2dM1​(s)=u2M1​(u)−∫0u​2sM1​(s)ds.

From that, you get the crucial monotonicity consequence.
Lemma: monotonicity of M1M1​ forces one sign of M3M3​

Since
M1(0)=0,
M1​(0)=0,

if M1M1​ is monotone on (0,U](0,U], then M1M1​ has one sign there:

    if M1M1​ is increasing, then M1(u)≥0M1​(u)≥0;

    if M1M1​ is decreasing, then M1(u)≤0M1​(u)≤0.

Now suppose M1M1​ is increasing. Then for 0≤s≤u0≤s≤u,
M1(s)≤M1(u),
M1​(s)≤M1​(u),

so
2∫0us M1(s) ds≤2∫0us M1(u) ds=u2M1(u).
2∫0u​sM1​(s)ds≤2∫0u​sM1​(u)ds=u2M1​(u).

Hence
M3(u)≥0.
M3​(u)≥0.

Similarly, if M1M1​ is decreasing, then
M1(s)≥M1(u),
M1​(s)≥M1​(u),

and since both sides are nonpositive, the same computation gives
M3(u)≤0.
M3​(u)≤0.

So:
M1 monotone ⟹M3 has the same sign as M1.
M1​ monotone ⟹M3​ has the same sign as M1​.​

And if M1M1​ is strictly monotone and not identically zero, then M3M3​ has strict one sign on (0,U](0,U].
Consequence for the quotient cusp lane

This collapses the earlier two-moment target to a single scalar target.

If M1M1​ is strictly monotone on the relevant quotient branch, then:

    T(u)=2M1(u)T(u)=2M1​(u) is strictly monotone, so there is no repeated tangent slope;

    M3M3​ has one sign, so
    S′(u)=4u3M3(u)
    S′(u)=u34​M3​(u)

    has one sign, hence the cusp-secant slope S(u)S(u) is strictly monotone, so there is no repeated secant slope from the cusp point;

    for

    H(u,v)=4∫uv​s(M1​(s)−M1​(u))ds,

    so strict monotonicity of M1M1​ forces H(u,v)H(u,v) to keep one sign for all v>uv>u. Thus the tangent at uu never passes through a later point.

Therefore:
strict monotonicity of M1(u)=∫0usK(s) ds is sufficient to make the quotient cusp branch globally sterile.
strict monotonicity of M1​(u)=∫0u​sK(s)ds is sufficient to make the quotient cusp branch globally sterile.​

That is a real improvement over the earlier target “control both M1M1​ and M3M3​.”
What this means for the next attack

The residual exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 lane has now reduced to one sharp theorem target:
prove a provenance-driven monotonicity law for M1(u).
prove a provenance-driven monotonicity law for M1​(u).​

Not for KK directly.
Not for the barycenter balance.
Not for primitive areas.
Just for
M1(u)=∫0us K(s) ds.
M1​(u)=∫0u​sK(s)ds.

If you can show M1M1​ is strictly monotone on the relevant quotient branch, then the whole local/nonlocal quotient-cusp recurrence problem dies at once.

So the next concrete attack I would make is:

    compute M1M1​ in terms of the exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 quotient data;

    look for an external mixed/canonical identity forcing the sign of
    M1′(u)=uK(u);
    M1′​(u)=uK(u);

    or, failing pointwise sign of KK, look for a one-sided comparison that still implies monotonicity of the cumulative moment M1M1​.

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

continue the math attack

There is one more clean push, and it is an important no-go:
on the quotient cusp branch, M1 is abstractly arbitrary.
on the quotient cusp branch, M1​ is abstractly arbitrary.​

So a monotonicity theorem for
M1(u)=∫0us K(s) ds
M1​(u)=∫0u​sK(s)ds

cannot come from quotient-cusp geometry alone. It must come from external provenance.

That is the sharpest further reduction I can justify.
1. Exact reconstruction from M1M1​

On the quotient cusp branch write
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G~(u)),u≥0,
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G
(u)),u≥0,

and define
T(u):=y′(u)x′(u)=G~′(u)2u,K(u):=dTdx(u).
T(u):=x′(u)y′(u)​=2uG
′(u)​,K(u):=dxdT​(u).

Earlier we proved
T(u)=2M1(u),M1(u):=∫0us K(s) ds.
T(u)=2M1​(u),M1​(u):=∫0u​sK(s)ds.

So M1M1​ is not just some derived moment. It is exactly half the tangent-slope function:
M1(u)=12 T(u).
M1​(u)=21​T(u).​

Then
y′(u)=2u T(u)=4u M1(u),
y′(u)=2uT(u)=4uM1​(u),

hence
y(u)=4∫0us M1(s) ds.
y(u)=4∫0u​sM1​(s)ds.​

And differentiating T=2M1T=2M1​ gives
2M1′(u)=2u K(u),
2M1′​(u)=2uK(u),

so
K(u)=M1′(u)u.
K(u)=uM1′​(u)​.​

Thus the entire quotient-cusp geometry is reconstructed from M1M1​:
x(u)=u2,T(u)=2M1(u),K(u)=M1′(u)u,y(u)=4∫0us M1(s) ds.
x(u)=u2,T(u)=2M1​(u),K(u)=uM1′​(u)​,y(u)=4∫0u​sM1​(s)ds.​

That is the key exact formula.
2. Cusp condition translated into M1M1​

For the quartic–sextic quotient cusp, we need
y(u)=c u3+O(u4),c≠0.
y(u)=cu3+O(u4),c=0.

Using the reconstruction formula, this is equivalent to
M1(u)=a u+O(u2),a≠0.
M1​(u)=au+O(u2),a=0.

Indeed, if
M1(u)=a u+O(u2),
M1​(u)=au+O(u2),

then
y(u)=4∫0us(au+O(u2)) ds=4a3u3+O(u4).
y(u)=4∫0u​s(au+O(u2))ds=34a​u3+O(u4).

So the quotient cusp imposes only:

    M1M1​ analytic,

    M1(0)=0M1​(0)=0,

    M1′(0)≠0M1′​(0)=0.

That is all.
3. Strong no-go theorem

Therefore:
every analytic germ M1(u) with M1(0)=0, M1′(0)≠0 occurs as the quotient-cusp moment function of some quartic–sextic germ.
every analytic germ M1​(u) with M1​(0)=0, M1′​(0)=0 occurs as the quotient-cusp moment function of some quartic–sextic germ.​

Equivalently:
the quotient-cusp branch carries one completely free analytic scalar germ M1.
the quotient-cusp branch carries one completely free analytic scalar germ M1​.​

This kills the hope that monotonicity of M1M1​ might be a formal consequence of the quotient-cusp normal form.

Because M1M1​ can be any analytic germ with nonzero linear term, you can realize:

    strictly increasing M1M1​,

    strictly decreasing M1M1​,

    M1M1​ with one interior critical point,

    M1M1​ with many oscillations,

    and hence all the corresponding good/bad tangent-return behaviors.

So the clean no-go statement is:
quotient-cusp geometry alone does not constrain M1 beyond M1(0)=0, M1′(0)≠0.
quotient-cusp geometry alone does not constrain M1​ beyond M1​(0)=0, M1′​(0)=0.​
4. Consequence for the attack

Earlier we reduced the residual exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 lane to:

    prove M1M1​ is strictly monotone.

This new result says:
that theorem cannot come from the cusp model itself.
that theorem cannot come from the cusp model itself.​

It has to come from something external to the bare quotient cusp:

    provenance,

    mixed/canonical structure,

    a nonlocal law along the actual branch,

    or an involutive/quotient-diagonal identity that constrains the allowed M1M1​-germs.

So the live theorem is now even sharper:
prove an external law that cuts down the free analytic germ M1.
prove an external law that cuts down the free analytic germ M1​.​
5. Best next target

Since M1M1​ is the true free quotient-cusp variable, the next useful theorem should be phrased directly in M1M1​, not in KK, TT, or yy.

The cleanest possibilities are now:
A. Pointwise determination near the residual slice

Something like
M1′(u)=H(u,external data),
M1′​(u)=H(u,external data),

or even
M1(u)=H(u,external data).
M1​(u)=H(u,external data).
B. Global/nonlocal monotonicity law

Something like
E[M1](u) is monotone/sign-definite
E[M1​](u) is monotone/sign-definite

along the actual quotient branch.
C. Involution-driven asymmetry

A theorem saying the actual residual provenance does not allow an arbitrary quotient germ M1M1​, but only a proper subclass, for example forbidding return of M1M1​-values.
6. The sharp update

So the attack has now reached this exact endpoint:
the residual exact fixed-shear Kv=0 lane is equivalent to one free analytic germ M1(u),
the residual exact fixed-shear Kv​=0 lane is equivalent to one free analytic germ M1​(u),​
and bare quotient-cusp geometry imposes only M1(0)=0, M1′(0)≠0.
and bare quotient-cusp geometry imposes only M1​(0)=0, M1′​(0)=0.​

Therefore the next live theorem is:
find the first external mixed/provenance law that constrains the free quotient germ M1.
find the first external mixed/provenance law that constrains the free quotient germ M1​.​

That is the sharpest continuation I can justify now.

Yes. There is one more clean reduction, and it is probably the sharpest endpoint of this Kv=0Kv​=0 quotient-cusp lane.

The residual exact fixed-shear problem is not just controlled by one free analytic germ M1M1​. It is equivalent to that germ, modulo the trivial nondegeneracy
M1(0)=0,M1′(0)≠0.
M1​(0)=0,M1′​(0)=0.

So the next theorem cannot be “prove something geometric about the quotient cusp.” It has to be:

Here is the exact reduction.

On the quotient cusp branch, with

produces a quartic–sextic quotient cusp germ. In that sense:

That lets us sharpen the no-go dramatically.

Any proposed local theorem on the exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 slice that is phrased only in terms of:

    the quotient cusp germ,

    finitely many derivatives of x,y,T,Kx,y,T,K,

    or finitely many derivatives of M1M1​,

is still too weak, because M1M1​ itself is arbitrary as an analytic germ.

So not only are the earlier geometric targets too weak:

    monotonicity of TT,

    split-point barycenter imbalance,

but in fact every finite-order local statement on the quotient cusp is too weak unless it imports external structure.

That is the new no-go theorem:
no finite-order local theorem on the bare quotient cusp can constrain the residual lane.
no finite-order local theorem on the bare quotient cusp can constrain the residual lane.​

This leaves only two live possibilities on the Kv=0Kv​=0 side.

First, a pointwise external law for M1M1​, meaning an identity like
F(u,external fields,M1(u),M1′(u),…,M1(r)(u))=0
F(u,external fields,M1​(u),M1′​(u),…,M1(r)​(u))=0

whose external fields are not themselves determined by the same quotient-cusp data.

Second, a nonlocal law for M1M1​, such as an integral identity, monotonicity principle, or transport law along the actual residual branch.

And because M1M1​ is the exact free quotient variable, we can now sharpen the order bookkeeping too.

If a pointwise external law determines M1M1​ algebraically,
M1(u)=H(u,external data),
M1​(u)=H(u,external data),

the lane closes immediately.

If it gives a first-order ODE
M1′(u)=G(u,external data,M1(u)),
M1′​(u)=G(u,external data,M1​(u)),

that is already strong enough to make tangent-return highly rigid, since repeated tangent slope is exactly repeated value of M1M1​.

So the next concrete attack I would make is:
look for the first external pointwise identity on the Kv=0 quotient slice whose highest-new content isolates M1 or M1′.
look for the first external pointwise identity on the Kv​=0 quotient slice whose highest-new content isolates M1​ or M1′​.​

That is better than looking for a law for KK, TT, or G~G
, because all of those are just differential shadows of the same germ M1M1​.

So the sharp strategic update is:
the exact fixed-shear Kv=0 residue has collapsed to one free analytic germ M1,

and the only live next theorem is an external pointwise or nonlocal law that cuts down M1.
and the only live next theorem is an external pointwise or nonlocal law that cuts down M1​.​

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify now.

I found a cleaner obstruction on the quotient-cusp lane.

The right object is still
M1(u)=∫0us K(s) ds,
M1​(u)=∫0u​sK(s)ds,

but the geometry is best phrased as a weighted Jensen problem for M1M1​, not as a sign problem for KK.

On the quotient cusp
Γ(u)=(u2,G~(u)),u≥0,
Γ(u)=(u2,G
(u)),u≥0,

we already have
T(u)=y′(u)x′(u)=2M1(u),y(u)=4∫0us M1(s) ds.
T(u)=x′(u)y′(u)​=2M1​(u),y(u)=4∫0u​sM1​(s)ds.

So the secant slope from the cusp point is
S(u)=y(u)u2=4u2∫0us M1(s) ds.
S(u)=u2y(u)​=u24​∫0u​sM1​(s)ds.

Now take two points 0<u<v0<u<v. A common tangent line at uu and vv is equivalent to:

    equal tangent slope,

T(u)=T(v)  ⟺  M1(u)=M1(v)=:m,
T(u)=T(v)⟺M1​(u)=M1​(v)=:m,

and

    the tangent at uu passes through vv, i.e.

y(v)−y(u)=T(u)(v2−u2).
y(v)−y(u)=T(u)(v2−u2).

Substituting the formulas gives
4∫uvs M1(s) ds=2m (v2−u2),
4∫uv​sM1​(s)ds=2m(v2−u2),

hence
m=2v2−u2∫uvs M1(s) ds.
m=v2−u22​∫uv​sM1​(s)ds.​

So a common tangent on the quotient cusp is exactly this:
M1(u)=M1(v)=2v2−u2∫uvs M1(s) ds.
M1​(u)=M1​(v)=v2−u22​∫uv​sM1​(s)ds.​

That is the cleanest exact criterion so far.

It says: the common endpoint value of M1M1​ must equal its weighted average on [u,v][u,v] with weight ss.

From this you get a sharp sufficient obstruction:
if M1 is strictly convex or strictly concave on [u,v], then no common tangent is possible.
if M1​ is strictly convex or strictly concave on [u,v], then no common tangent is possible.​

Reason: if M1(u)=M1(v)=mM1​(u)=M1​(v)=m and M1M1​ is strictly convex, then
M1(s)<m(u<s<v),
M1​(s)<m(u<s<v),

so its positive weighted average is strictly less than mm, contradiction. If M1M1​ is strictly concave, the weighted average is strictly greater than mm, again contradiction.

So the quotient-cusp lane reduces to this very concrete theorem target:
prove provenance-driven strict convexity or strict concavity of M1 on the relevant quotient branch.
prove provenance-driven strict convexity or strict concavity of M1​ on the relevant quotient branch.​

That is strictly weaker than proving monotonicity of M1M1​, and stronger than the older primitive-area and barycenter formulations.

There is also a parallel formula for cusp secants:
S(u)=4u2∫0us M1(s) ds,
S(u)=u24​∫0u​sM1​(s)ds,

so SS is itself a weighted average of M1M1​ over [0,u][0,u]. Thus any external law that makes M1M1​ one-sidedly ordered against its weighted averages would also kill repeated cusp-secant slopes.

The catch is the same as before: abstractly, this is still too weak. Since M1M1​ is an arbitrary analytic germ on the bare quotient cusp, one can choose nonconvex M1M1​ realizing the bad geometry. So this does not close the lane by itself.

But it gives a sharper next attack than “control KK”:

    not sign of KK,

    not primitive-area balance,

    not split barycenters,

but:
find an external mixed/provenance law forcing M1′′ to have one sign
find an external mixed/provenance law forcing M1′′​ to have one sign​

or more directly strict convexity/concavity of M1M1​.

That is the cleanest continuation I can justify.

Yes. There is one more clean push, and I think it is the sharpest local reduction on the quotient-cusp lane.

M1​(u)=M1​(v)=m,

one has
M1(s)<m(u<s<v),
M1​(s)<m(u<s<v),

hence

∫uv​M1​(s)dωu,v​(s)<m,

contradicting the common tangent condition.

Likewise, if M1M1​ is strictly concave on [u,v][u,v], then
M1(s)>m(u<s<v),
M1​(s)>m(u<s<v),

so the weighted average is >m>m, again impossible.

Therefore:
strict convexity or strict concavity of M1 on a candidate interval rules out common tangent return.
strict convexity or strict concavity of M1​ on a candidate interval rules out common tangent return.​

This is better than the earlier monotonicity target:

    monotonicity of M1M1​ was sufficient,

    but strict convexity/concavity is a different and often more accessible sufficient condition.

There is also a parallel statement for cusp secants. Since
S(u)=4u2∫0us M1(s) ds=2∫0uM1(s) 2su2 ds,
S(u)=u24​∫0u​sM1​(s)ds=2∫0u​M1​(s)u22s​ds,

the cusp-secant slope is itself a weighted average of M1M1​ over [0,u][0,u]. So any provenance law forcing M1M1​ to sit strictly above or below its ss-weighted averages would also kill repeated cusp-secant slopes.

So the next clean theorem target is now:
provenance-driven strict Jensen inequality for M1.
provenance-driven strict Jensen inequality for M1​.​

A theorem of either of these forms would be enough:

or more generally

M1​ lies strictly above or below all its v2−u22s​-weighted interval averages.

That is now the sharpest local target I can justify.

The honest ceiling is the same as before: abstractly, this still does not close the lane, because the bare quotient cusp lets M1M1​ be any analytic germ with
M1(0)=0,M1′(0)≠0.
M1​(0)=0,M1′​(0)=0.

So strict convexity/concavity cannot come from quotient-cusp geometry alone.

But this reduction is useful because it pinpoints exactly what an external mixed/provenance theorem would need to prove.

1. Exact Jensen-gap reformulation

On the quotient cusp branch, set
x=u2,N(x):=M1(x).

    can NN take the same value at two points while its interval average equals that same value?

2. Strong no-go: one sign change of K′K′ is not enough

The earlier sufficient criterion was:

    if NN is strictly convex or strictly concave, no recurrence.

Since
N′′(x)=14uK′(u),x=u2,
N′′(x)=4u1​K′(u),x=u2,

that corresponds to K′K′ keeping one sign.

The next natural hope would be: maybe recurrence is still impossible if K′K′ changes sign only once. That is false.

Take the cubic

N(0)=N(1)=0,

N′′(x)=6x−3,
N′′(x)=6x−3,

so N′′N′′ changes sign exactly once on (0,1)(0,1).

Translated back to the quotient-cusp notation, this means:
a single sign change of N′′, equivalently of K′, is already compatible with recurrence.
a single sign change of N′′, equivalently of K′, is already compatible with recurrence.​

So any theorem of the form
“K′ changes sign at most once”
“K′ changes sign at most once”

is still too weak.

That is a real sharpening.
3. What this means

This kills another whole class of plausible local attacks.

It is not enough to prove:

    K′K′ has at most one zero;

    N′′N′′ has at most one sign change;

    or any similarly weak “single-inflection” statement.

The cubic model shows that one inflection is already enough to realize the exact Jensen equality needed for common tangent return.

So the next live theorem has to be stronger than curvature-sign-change counting.
4. The sharp next target

The next useful theorem is now:
strict Jensen-gap sign theorem for the actual provenance-driven N.
strict Jensen-gap sign theorem for the actual provenance-driven N.​

Not “NN is convex.”
Not “K′K′ has one sign.”
Not “K′K′ has at most one sign change.”

But rather a theorem directly of the form:

JN​[x1​,x2​]

has a definite sign whenever
N(x1)=N(x2)
N(x1​)=N(x2​)

on a candidate interval.

This is stronger than convexity, but weaker than full determination of NN. And it is exactly the functional that the bad geometry needs to vanish.

So the next attack I would make is:

Here is a genuinely sharper reduction.

A common tangent on a symmetric interval is controlled by two independent Peano-kernel functionals of the midpoint-odd and midpoint-even parts of N′′N′′. That splits the geometry cleanly into an endpoint-equality obstruction and a Jensen-gap obstruction.

This is better than the previous “midpoint-even curvature average” statement.
1. Center the interval and split N′′N′′

    the new result applies to a fixed center and says repeated arbitrarily small symmetric recurrences force
    K≡0
    K≡0

    locally.

So the local residual lane is now very rigid:

    no “spray” of small symmetric recurrences can emanate from one center unless the branch is locally flat.

That is a real local sterility theorem.
6. What remains

This still does not fully close the lane, because a bad branch could in principle use:

    nonsymmetric intervals,

    or symmetric intervals with moving centers mn→m0mn​→m0​.

rule out moving-center symmetric recurrences.
rule out moving-center symmetric recurrences.​

That is the natural next step from here.

fixed-center local clouds of symmetric common tangents are impossible unless K≡0.
fixed-center local clouds of symmetric common tangents are impossible unless K≡0.​

Here is the cleanest next push I can justify.

The moving-center symmetric problem reduces to a single analytic elimination problem.

That is sharper than the earlier fixed-center sterility theorem.
1. Package symmetric common tangents into two analytic equations

For a symmetric interval [m−L,m+L][m−L,m+L], define

2. Fixed-center sterility becomes zz-fiber sterility

The earlier fixed-center theorem now reads:

    for fixed mm, if there are infinitely many zn↓0zn​↓0 with
    A(m,zn)=B(m,zn)=0,
    A(m,zn​)=B(m,zn​)=0,

3. The moving-center problem is now an elimination problem

What remains is the case where
(mn,zn)→(m0,0),zn>0,A(mn,zn)=B(mn,zn)=0,

This is no longer a geometric problem about secants or tangents. It is an analytic common-zero problem for the pair
A(m,z),B(m,z).
A(m,z),B(m,z).

Generically, two analytic equations in two variables have only isolated common zeros. So a local cloud of moving-center symmetric recurrences is already highly nongeneric.

That gives the right new theorem target:
prove that the analytic pair (A,B) has no nontrivial common branch near (m0,0).
prove that the analytic pair (A,B) has no nontrivial common branch near (m0​,0).​
4. The resultant reduction

This can be phrased as a one-variable obstruction.

    A(m,z)=B(m,z)=0;

This is much sharper than anything we had before.

It says the only way a local cloud of moving-center symmetric recurrences can survive is if the odd-channel obstruction AA and even-channel obstruction BB satisfy a genuine analytic dependence in zz.

That is a very rigid condition.

Yes. There is a real next theorem now, and it is stronger than just “local clouds are impossible.”

The exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 quotient-cusp lane is now reduced to a discrete global-return problem.

away from locally affine pieces, common tangent pairs on the quotient cusp branch form a discrete set.​

And on every compact subarc, there are only finitely many such pairs.

1. The analytic common-tangent map

On the quotient cusp branch
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G~(u)),u>0,
Γ(u)=(x(u),y(u))=(u2,G
(u)),u>0,

define
T(u):=y′(u)x′(u)=G~′(u)2u,
T(u):=x′(u)y′(u)​=2uG
′(u)​,

and for 0<u<v0<u<v,
H(u,v):=y(v)−y(u)−T(u)(x(v)−x(u)).
H(u,v):=y(v)−y(u)−T(u)(x(v)−x(u)).

A common tangent at uu and vv is exactly
T(v)−T(u)=0,H(u,v)=0.
T(v)−T(u)=0,H(u,v)=0.

So define the analytic map
F(u,v):=(T(v)−T(u), H(u,v))
F(u,v):=(T(v)−T(u),H(u,v))

on the open wedge
Ω:={(u,v):0<u<v}.
Ω:={(u,v):0<u<v}.

Then the common-tangent set is
C:={(u,v)∈Ω:F(u,v)=0}.
C:={(u,v)∈Ω:F(u,v)=0}.
2. Local sterility implies discreteness

From the previous theorem:

    if (un,vn)∈C(un​,vn​)∈C with
    un→u0,vn→u0,
    un​→u0​,vn​→u0​,

    then TT is locally constant near u0u0​, hence K≡0K≡0 there.

So on any interval where the quotient branch is genuinely nontrivial,
K≢0,
K≡0,

there is no accumulation of CC onto the diagonal u=vu=v.

A second easy observation is that if
(un,vn)∈C,(un,vn)→(u0,v0)∈Ω,
(un​,vn​)∈C,(un​,vn​)→(u0​,v0​)∈Ω,

then by analyticity
F(u0,v0)=0.
F(u0​,v0​)=0.

So any accumulation inside ΩΩ would give a positive-dimensional analytic zero set through (u0,v0)(u0​,v0​). But a local analytic branch of zeros through (u0,v0)(u0​,v0​) would, by following it toward v−u→0v−u→0, contradict the local sterility theorem unless TT were locally constant on one side. Thus on a genuinely non-affine quotient branch, CC has no interior accumulation either.

Hence:
if K≢0 on a quotient-cusp arc, then C is discrete in Ω.
if K≡0 on a quotient-cusp arc, then C is discrete in Ω.​

And therefore, on every compact rectangle
{(u,v):u0≤u<v≤v0}⊂Ω,
{(u,v):u0​≤u<v≤v0​}⊂Ω,

3. This gives a finite-core reduction on the quotient cusp

So the residual exact fixed-shear Kv=0Kv​=0 lane is now not a local instability problem. It is a finite global-return problem.

On any compact quotient-cusp branch away from locally affine pieces, there are only finitely many candidate common tangent pairs.

That means the whole residual lane now reduces to:
rule out isolated global tangent returns on the one-sided quotient cusp branch.
rule out isolated global tangent returns on the one-sided quotient cusp branch.​

This is a real simplification. It says there is no continuum of bad pairs, no bubbling, no nearby re-entry cloud. Any surviving obstruction is a finite combinatorial object.

That is exactly the kind of reduction that could potentially be fed into the finite-core / first-return style arguments from elsewhere in the draft.
4. A sharper scalar reduction on the discrete set

We can also package the discrete return problem in the M1M1​-language.

Recall
T(u)=2M1(u),M1(u):=∫0usK(s) ds.

and
m=2v2−u2∫uvs M1(s) ds.

So isolated common tangent pairs are exactly isolated pairs (u,v)(u,v) where:

    M1M1​ takes the same value at uu and vv,

    and that common value equals the ss-weighted average of M1M1​ on [u,v][u,v].

Since the bad set is discrete, the quotient-cusp lane has now become an isolated weighted-Jensen return problem for one scalar function M1M1​.

That is probably the best exact scalar reduction available.

B. Global one-sided law for the Jensen return functional

on the equal-value locus

M1​(u)=M1​(v).

Since the bad set is finite on compact arcs, one may be able to choose a first/outermost/nearest bad pair and derive a contradiction by minimality.

That would connect nicely to the finite-core extraction style you wanted other chats to pursue.

4. Endpoint sign near critical points

There is also a clean local sign at branch birth.

Suppose an equal-value branch emanates from a nondegenerate critical point u0u0​ of M1M1​, with nearby equal-value pairs

    F(u,v)>0

    near that endpoint;

    if u0u0​ is a local minimum, then M1(s)−m<0M1​(s)−m<0, hence
    F(u,v)<0.
    F(u,v)<0.

Thus the sign of FF near a branch endpoint is determined by the type of critical point:

max endpoint ⇒F>0 nearby,min endpoint ⇒F<0 nearby.​

Combined with monotonicity, this gives a complete branch classification in principle.
5. Branch classification

Let a connected equal-value branch of ΣΣ run between two critical points of M1M1​ (or a critical point and a boundary). Then:

    if both ends are born from maxima, FF has the same positive sign at both ends, so there is no zero;

    if both ends are born from minima, FF has the same negative sign at both ends, so there is no zero;

    if one end is a max and the other a min, then by continuity and strict monotonicity, there is exactly one zero of FF, hence exactly one common tangent pair on that branch.

So the residual quotient-cusp problem is now extremely concrete:
common tangent pairs occur exactly on max-to-min equal-value branches of M1.
common tangent pairs occur exactly on max-to-min equal-value branches of M1​.​

6. What this means for the next attack

This changes the target a lot.

You do not need to rule out arbitrary isolated bad pairs one by one.

It is enough to prove one of the following:

    equal-value branches of M1M1​ can never connect a local maximum to a local minimum;

    or the actual provenance-driven M1M1​ has only one type of critical point on the relevant quotient branch;

    or there is a global ordering of critical values that prevents max-to-min branches.

Any one of those would close the residual quotient-cusp lane.

So the next attack I would make is:

2. Sign near a branch endpoint is determined by the endpoint critical-point type

Suppose one endpoint of the branch corresponds to a critical point cc of M1M1​, with
M1(c)=m∗.
M1​(c)=m∗​.

Then:

    if cc is a local maximum, for m<m∗m<m∗​ close to m∗m∗​ the pair lies on the two sides of cc, and
    M1(s)−m>0
    M1​(s)−m>0

    on the interior, hence
    F(m)>0;
    F(m)>0;

    if cc is a local minimum, for m>m∗m>m∗​ close to m∗m∗​,
    M1(s)−m<0
    M1​(s)−m<0

    on the interior, hence
    F(m)<0.
    F(m)<0.

So the sign of FF near an endpoint is determined solely by whether that endpoint critical point is a max or a min.
3. Exact classification of bad branches

Since FF is strictly decreasing in mm, it can have a zero on a branch iff it is positive near the lower-mm endpoint and negative near the upper-mm endpoint.

Therefore:
an equal-value branch contains a common tangent pair iff its lower-level endpoint is a local maximum and its upper-level endpoint is a local minimum.
an equal-value branch contains a common tangent pair iff its lower-level endpoint is a local maximum and its upper-level endpoint is a local minimum.​

And when this happens, the zero of FF is unique.

Equivalently:
common tangents on the quotient cusp occur exactly on “inverted” equal-value branches:
common tangents on the quotient cusp occur exactly on “inverted” equal-value branches:​
the smaller critical value is attained at a max, and the larger critical value at a min.
the smaller critical value is attained at a max, and the larger critical value at a min.​

This is the sharpest discrete classification I’ve gotten.
4. Why this is a real improvement

Earlier we had:

    bad pairs are discrete;

    each equal-value branch has at most one bad pair.

Now we have the exact yes/no criterion:

    max–max branch: no zero;

    min–min branch: no zero;

    lower=min, upper=max branch: no zero;

    lower=max, upper=min branch: exactly one zero.

So the quotient-cusp lane has reduced completely to a critical-value ordering problem for M1M1​.

That is much cleaner than the previous integral/barycenter language.
5. This matches the counterexample

The earlier explicit counterexample on the bare quotient cusp is no longer mysterious.

It must come from exactly one of these inverted branches:
a local maximum of M1M1​ with smaller value than a local minimum elsewhere on the branch.

So the abstract geometry is now fully understood.
6. The next live theorem target

This means the next thing to prove is not another local slope or curvature estimate.

It is:
prove that on the actual provenance-driven Kv=0 quotient branch, inverted max/min equal-value branches cannot occur.
prove that on the actual provenance-driven Kv​=0 quotient branch, inverted max/min equal-value branches cannot occur.​

Any theorem implying one of the following would close the lane:

    local maxima of M1M1​ always have larger value than later local minima;

    or critical values of M1M1​ are ordered monotonically by type along the branch;

    or the actual provenance forces a Reeb-graph ordering of M1M1​ with no max-below-min inversions.

That is now the sharpest possible target on this lane.

So the strategic update is:
the exact fixed-shear Kv=0 residual lane has reduced to a single question: does M1 admit inverted max/min equal-value branches?
the exact fixed-shear Kv​=0 residual lane has reduced to a single question: does M1​ admit inverted max/min equal-value branches?​

That is where I would push next.

2. Envelope formulation

3. Minimal inversion principle

    equivalently, on [ci,cj][ci​,cj​], all intermediate maxima are ≤μi≤μi​ and all intermediate minima are ≥μj≥μj​.

So a minimal bad branch corresponds to a clean staircase inversion:
a lower max followed later by a higher min, with no intermediate critical values entering the forbidden strip.

That is the finite-core version of the last theorem.

So the next theorem does not need to rule out every possible inversion. It is enough to rule out this clean minimal one.

2. Why the raw critical-value list is still too redundant

The raw sequence (μi)(μi​) contains lots of irrelevant extrema.

For example:

    a local max whose value is below an earlier larger max does not create a new upper barrier;

    a local min whose value is above a later smaller min does not create a new lower barrier.

Those dominated extrema do not affect whether a max-to-min inversion branch can survive globally.

So the right object is not the raw sequence, but the pruned envelope sequence.
3. Reduced stack / envelope sequence

Define the running upper envelope of maxima and the running lower envelope of minima. Equivalently, perform the standard monotone-stack pruning:

    keep only maxima that are strictly larger than all earlier kept maxima;

    keep only minima that are strictly smaller than all later kept minima.

After pruning, one gets a reduced alternating sequence of critical points
c^1<c^2<⋯<c^M

    kept maxima have strictly increasing “record height” left-to-right;

    kept minima have strictly increasing “record floor” left-to-right when read as future lower envelope;

    every deleted extremum is dominated by a previously kept upper or later kept lower barrier.

This reduced sequence is the true combinatorial core.
4. Sharp reduction

The residual quotient-cusp lane should now reduce to:
a common tangent pair exists iff the reduced stack contains an inversion μ^max⁡<μ^min⁡ with the max earlier than the min.

In other words, after pruning all dominated extrema, the only surviving obstruction is a clean staircase inversion:

This is stronger than the earlier raw max/min inversion statement, because it removes all fake counterexamples coming from irrelevant oscillations.

I think this is the correct finite-core model of the quotient-cusp lane.
5. What this buys you

If this reduction is right, then the residual Kv=0Kv​=0 lane is no longer an analytic return problem at all. It is a finite ordered critical-value problem for one scalar function M1M1​.

Then to close the lane, it would be enough to prove one of the following about the actual provenance-driven M1M1​:

    the reduced stack never contains an earlier max below a later min;

    or the kept maxima always dominate all later kept minima;

    or equivalently, the reduced upper envelope lies everywhere above the reduced lower envelope.

This is exactly the kind of theorem a global provenance law might plausibly imply.

That is standard 1D contour-tree / merge-tree behavior, but I have not fully formalized it from the draft’s current notation. So I would state this as the next precise theorem to prove, not as already finished.

4. Why this is useful

This tells you exactly what kind of theorem would now close the quotient-cusp lane.

You do not need to understand all of M1M1​. It is enough to prove any one of the following at every level mm:

    weighted excursion areas cannot balance to zero across an alternating chain;

    the positive excursion areas dominate the negative ones, or vice versa;

    or minimal zero-sum chains are impossible.

5. Relation to the critical-value picture

The previous max/min inversion picture is still there, but this is more exact.

A critical-value inversion means there exists some level mm where the excursion structure of M1−mM1​−m has both positive and negative components in the right order. The new theorem says a common tangent appears only if the weighted areas of those excursions sum to zero.

So the critical-value ordering is the coarse necessary condition; the excursion-chain balance is the exact condition.
